<div id="review_542446870" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542446870" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542446870?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542446870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12529988711041301968"><br/>One of the joys of reading chaotically, picking up books from the TBR stack at the whim of the moment and not according to some master plan, is to discover that succesive reads turn out to be related after all. <b> The Great Gatsby </b> is concerned with the Great American Dream - that success is waiting right around the corner for anyone determined enough to reach for it. <b> Butcher's Crossing </b> is about another facet of the Great American Dream, the myth of the pristine land, a Garden of Eden where Man </span>
  <span id="freeText12529988711041301968" style="display:none"><br />One of the joys of reading chaotically, picking up books from the TBR stack at the whim of the moment and not according to some master plan, is to discover that succesive reads turn out to be related after all. <b> The Great Gatsby </b> is concerned with the Great American Dream - that success is waiting right around the corner for anyone determined enough to reach for it. <b> Butcher's Crossing </b> is about another facet of the Great American Dream, the myth of the pristine land, a Garden of Eden where Man can go to find beauty, peace and dignity.<br /><br />Will Andrews is a child of the modern world, growing up in Boston around 1870 in a reasonably wealthy family. His imagination is fired up by the discourses of Ralph Waldo Emerson and he decides to leave Harvard and strike West, not in search of wealth or fame, but chasing the meaning of life and spiritual fulfillment.<br /><br /><i> At the gate of the forest, the surprised man of the world is forced to leave his city estimates of great and small, wise and foolish. The knapsack of custom falls off his back with the first step he takes into these precincts. Here is sanctity which shames our religions, and reality which discredits our heroes. Here we find Nature to be the circumstance which dwarfs every other circumstance, and judges like a god all men that come to her. [Emerson] </i><br /><br />His destination is Butcher's Crossing, a dusty settlement on the Kansas frontier, catering to buffalo hunters. The harsh travelling conditions, the gruff locals and the dingy, derelict houses do little to curb his enthusiasm, his eyes ever turning towards the westward prairie. He turns down an offer to join in the profitable business of tanning buffalo hides, prefering to look out for a guide into the wilderness:<br /><br /><i> It was a freedom and a goodness, a hope and a vigor that he perceived to underlie all the familiar things of his life, which were not free or good or hopeful or vigorous. What he sought was the source and preserver of his world, a world which seemed to turn ever in fear away from its source, rather than search it out, as the prairie grass around him sent down its fibered roots into the rich, dank dampness, the Wildness, and thereby renew itself, year after year. </i><br /><br />His moderate savings enable him to finance a hunting expedition towards a secret location in the the Colorado mountains, a pet project of Miller - a lone wolf hunter with a difficult personality but with 20 years experience in the field. The team is completed with Miller's one handed partner Charley Hoge, a drunken Bible thumper and by a hired hand - a professional skinner of a contrarian disposition, always grumbling and challenging Miller's leadership.<br /><br />The novel really takes off once the expedition sets out on the trackless prairie, with Williams wonderful prose capturing both the 'true grit' of saddle sores, debilitating tiredness, thirst, mind numbing boredom, and the poetry of the boundless vistas, the sea like quality of being at the center of the universe and moving in a timeless bubble outside the reality of civilized East Coast. Miller is like a force of nature, pushing all of them forward mercilessly, reading the lay of the land, the sun and the winds with consummate skill. What is missing in this landscape is the object of the hunt - the buffalo has already been hunted to near extinction and is present only as mounds of white bones or the occasional wounded stray. Miller's obsession with the herd hidden in his secret mountain valley reminds of Melville and his Captain Ahab chasing another impossible dream.<br /><br />Miller's dream though turns out to be true, as the heavy bull driven cart comes at last to the high pass opening into a vision of paradise, a veritable Shangri-la hidden from covetous eyes, as perfect a camping place as I ever encountered on my own mountain treks:<br /><br /><i> A long narrow valley, flat as the top of a table, wound among the mountains. Lush grass grew on the bed of the valley, and waved gently in the breeze as far as the eye could see. A quietness seemed to rise from the valley; it was the quietness, the stillness, the absolute calm of a land where no human foot had touched. Andrews found that despite his exhaustion he was holding his breath; he expelled the air from his lungs as gently as he could, so as not to disturb the silence. </i><br /><br />As a sidenote, I'm not familiar with the detailed history of the Colorado territories in the XIX century, but it seems to me Williams is ignoring completely the Native American angle. A single instance of meeting a destitute Indian family scraping a meagre living on the plains makes it seem like the author deliberately ignored their historical presence as outside the scope of his novel - he needed a pristine setting in order to make his point.<br /><br />The promised herd of buffalo is here in great numbers, resplendent in their autumn coats, well fed on the bountiful grass and as yet unafraid of the danger humans represent. Young Andrews education turns to a bloody and gruesome page, as the idillic landscape becomes a scene of indiscriminate slaughter and Miller's goal of wiping out the herd completely is revealed as the spirit of the modern world that gets drunk on power and immediate profit without any thought for long term consequences or preservation of resources ['Drill Baby, Drill!']. Miller's unhinged mind is blind to all appeals at reason and moderation, as the gathered skins far outstrip the carrying capacity of their cart. Nature or karma strikes back, and the expedition is trapped in the high mountain valley by an early snowstorm.<br /><br />Once again, the writing knocked me down as it describes the struggle for survival and the tensions between the four members of the team, the long tedious months of being cooped up in an improvised shelter, the lack of even the most basic comforts. A weary and disillusioned Andrews comes down in the spring and slowly makes his way back to Butcher's Crossing for a big finale in which the falsity, the destructive nature of the American Dream is reaffirmed in an emphatic way.<br /><br /><i> Young people', McDonald said contemptuously. 'You always think there's something to find out. Well, there's nothing. You get born, and you nurse on lies, and you get weaned on lies, and you learn fancier lies in school. You live all your life on lies, and then maybe when you're ready to die, it comes to you - that there's nothing, nothing but yourself and what you could have done. Only you ain't done it, because the lies told you there was something else. Then you know you could of had the world, because you're the only one that knows the secret; only the it's too late. You're too old. </i><br /><br />This is a bleak revelation, but I have the feeling a necessary one. The lessons have been painful, but Andrews is still young and a small hope exists that he will step into his next adventure with his eyes open. Being more circumspect does not mean giving up altogether. At least this is how I like to look at the outcome.<br /><br />I am surprised the novel is not better known, it is probably the most literate, thoughtful and brutal analysis of the Western myth I've come across since watching Jeremiah Johnson at the Cinemateque. I can see how modern writers like Larry McMurtry or Cormac mcCarthy may have been influenced in their approach by this definitely unromantic look at the Frontier.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12529988711041301968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1388354191" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1388354191" />
    <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk"><img alt="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592525719p2/8930751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1388354191?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk">ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099589679" href="/book/show/18395049-butcher-s-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1388354191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12926265888480944097">Wonderful book. Read it. Americana at its best. "Stoner" was a book that made me look into my life as I am. "Butcher's Crossing" took me back to my youth when I wandered, and lived in my tent in the mountains, became lost in a snow storm in the North. But more than anything it took me back to this: <br/><br/>"He could hardly recall, now, the passion that had drawn him to this room and this flesh, as if by a subtle magnetism; nor could he recall the force of that other passion which had impelled him halfwa</span>
  <span id="freeText12926265888480944097" style="display:none">Wonderful book. Read it. Americana at its best. "Stoner" was a book that made me look into my life as I am. "Butcher's Crossing" took me back to my youth when I wandered, and lived in my tent in the mountains, became lost in a snow storm in the North. But more than anything it took me back to this: <br /><br />"He could hardly recall, now, the passion that had drawn him to this room and this flesh, as if by a subtle magnetism; nor could he recall the force of that other passion which had impelled him halfway across a continent into a wilderness where he had dreamed he could find, as in a vision, his unalterable self. Almost without regret, he could admit now the vanity from which those passions had sprung."<br /><br />The "unalterable self", of course is found to be "a nothingness" and we continue. We can continue into the unknown being certain that there is no meaning or we can return to what we know and still be nothing. Just as Stoner accepts his fate 'stoically' (at least the popular view of stoicism), so the young William Andrews accepts his losses and carries on with no sense of regret. <br /><br />Williams wrote true classics. His sentences often read like the Old Testament, reminding me of Cormac McCarthy's style. Perhaps McCarthy's style with Wallace Stegner's thoughtfulness. Indeed, I should not be surprised to learn that McCarthy had read Butcher's Crossing prior to writing Blood Meridian. There are many commonalities. <br /><br />Strangely, for myself, this book touched many memories of dreams, of events and of emotions of my youth, as cited above. Even my philosophical beliefs were brought into play. Finally, I think of one of my great grandfathers who rode away from home one day in Montana in the 1890s. He was never heard of again. Such was the nature of the times. Such is the nature of this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12926265888480944097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1597391066" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1597391066" />
    <a title="Cathrine ☯️ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22600410-cathrine"><img alt="Cathrine ☯️ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582396518p2/22600410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1597391066?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathrine ☯️ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathrine ☯️ " href="/user/show/22600410-cathrine">Cathrine ☯️ </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22600410-cathrine?shelf=group-challenge">group-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1597391066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13645888542219777489"><b>5★</b><br/>In his third year at Harvard, Will Andrews leaves his studies to go west. After hearing a lecture by Ralph Waldo Emerson his quest is to find and experience his <b>“unadulterated self.”</b> The year is 1873 as he arrives in Butcher’s Crossing Colorado. He decides to fund a buffalo hunt as a way <b>“to see as much of the country as I can. . . I want to get to know it.”</b> I picture that scene in <i>Dances With Wolves</i> when the character played by Kevin Costner relates that he wants to see the West before it is </span>
  <span id="freeText13645888542219777489" style="display:none"><b>5★</b><br />In his third year at Harvard, Will Andrews leaves his studies to go west. After hearing a lecture by Ralph Waldo Emerson his quest is to find and experience his <b>“unadulterated self.”</b> The year is 1873 as he arrives in Butcher’s Crossing Colorado. He decides to fund a buffalo hunt as a way <b>“to see as much of the country as I can. . . I want to get to know it.”</b> I picture that scene in <i>Dances With Wolves</i> when the character played by Kevin Costner relates that he wants to see the West before it is all gone, and then later when he and his tribal friends come upon the scene of buffalo slaughtered for their hide and left to rot. As Michelle Latiolais writes in her excellent introduction, <b>"Andrews seeks the wilderness so the he can be <i>'a part and parcel of God, free and unconfined’</i>; what he will later encounter in nature is more akin to the malice of an Old Testament God.”</b> <br />Later as he participates in the greedy slaughter of five thousand bison he <b>“did not know who he was, or where he went.”</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/51229.John_Williams" title="John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">John Williams</a>, author of another favorite of mine <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/166997.Stoner" title="Stoner by John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Stoner</a>, gives us a harrowing, unadulterated, and unromanticized vision of unchecked United States westward expansion, pillaging, and slaughter. This is not the glorified version of <i>How The West Was Won</i> which so many still cling to today and it's a part of our collective soul as a nation. It was a tough read. I’m always saddened by the epic tragedy of what happened to our native tribes and the animal they so desperately depended upon. But I’m pleased that this masterful writer gave us this authentic American western novel.<br /><br />To end on a positive note, as I was nearing the conclusion (so grateful for the timing here), a 90 second video by The Nature Conservancy popped up in my newsfeed about continuing efforts to reestablish these magnificent beasts to the plains. This is in recognition of President Obama’s signing of bipartisan legislation naming the bison America’s 1st national mammal on May 9, 2016 (link below). Perhaps this will insure that they never appear on the extinct species list.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/GetHistoryNow/videos/vb.429865680551904/486561111549027/?type=2&amp;theater">https://www.facebook.com/GetHistoryNo...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13645888542219777489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_117479524" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/117479524" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117479524?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2010">finished-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117479524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4748491210829961357">Penned in 1960, John Williams' BUTCHER'S CROSSING anticipates and in many ways eclipses Cormac McCarthy's western works because it not only nails the rapacious greed of the buffalo hunters it describes, it reaches for more abstract and troubling themes that go to the very essence of man and his place in the world.<br/><br/>Will Andrews, the protagonist, is but a 23-year-old preacher's son when he shows up in Butcher's Crossing with money and a dream in hand. He winds up payrolling a buffalo hunt to a hidd</span>
  <span id="freeText4748491210829961357" style="display:none">Penned in 1960, John Williams' BUTCHER'S CROSSING anticipates and in many ways eclipses Cormac McCarthy's western works because it not only nails the rapacious greed of the buffalo hunters it describes, it reaches for more abstract and troubling themes that go to the very essence of man and his place in the world.<br /><br />Will Andrews, the protagonist, is but a 23-year-old preacher's son when he shows up in Butcher's Crossing with money and a dream in hand. He winds up payrolling a buffalo hunt to a hidden valley known only to a hunter named Miller. Along with his whiskey-swilling, Bible-thumping driver, Charley Hogue, and a sardonic skinner named Schneider, Miller leads Will and the others across the spectacular western landscape to their fates in a land of milk, honey, and blood. Along the way, you are treated to some fine nature writing, such as this:<br /><br />"For a long time after he had bedded down, Will Andrews listened to the silence around him. For a while the acrid smell of the smothered pine log's burning warmed his nostrils; then the wind shifted and he could no longer smell the smoke or hear the heavy breaths of the sleeping men around him. He turned so that he faced the side of the mountain over which they had traveled. From the darkness that clung about the earth he lifted his gaze and followed the dim outlines of particular trees as they rose from the darkness and gradually gained distinctness against the deep blue cloudless sky that twinkled with the light of the clear stars. Even with an extra blanket on his bedroll, he was chilled; he could see the gray cloud of his breath as he breathed the sharp night air. His eyes closed upon the image of a tall conical pine tree outlined blackly against the luminous sky, and despite the cold he slept soundly until morning."<br /><br />The hunt scenes and the constant personal battles between the fire of Miller and the ice of Schneider give the book its brutal punch and provide the richness from which the theme will grow as the narrative grinds to its relentless conclusion. It's no coincidence that Miller's name is one letter away from "killer." An Ahab of the Mountain Valleys, Miller knows his prey so well that the beasts stand no chance. The blood and gore are smeared upon the entire party, and though Will spends one memorable scene in a freezing cold river trying to scrape it off, it is as much a legacy to each individual as is Lady Macbeth's stained hands.<br /><br />Of course, despite being master of the bison, even Miller cannot subdue nature, and nature collects her due -- abundantly, , as is her wont, when human folly and greed allow. The simple yet epic depictions of the fall and winter, of how frail these four men are in the face of it, and of their struggle to return with their furred fortunes, are both appalling and gripping.<br /><br />In addition to the moral issues, Williams plays the coming-of-age card as well, constantly using darkness and light with their ancient, metaphoric grips on our imaginations and fears. Here Will listens to the frightful rantings of a merchant named McDonald:<br /><br />"'Well, there's nothing,' McDonald said. 'You get born and you nurse on lies, and you get weaned on lies, and you learn fancier lies in school. You live all your life on lies, and then maybe when you're ready to die, it comes to you -- that there's nothing, nothing but yourself and what you could have done. Only you ain't done it, because the lies told you there was something else. Then you know you could of had the world, because you're the only one that knows the secret; only then it's too late. You're too old.'<br /><br />'No,' Andrews said. A vague terror crept from the darkness that surrounded them, and tightened his voice. 'That's not the way it is.'"<br /><br />Let's see: a literary western with great writing, realistic and rich descriptions, epic characterization, deep and disturbing themes, coming-of-age angst, and the audacity to flirt with the meaning of life. <br /><br />If you've got the patience for the long ride out and the long ride back, you might want to deal in. And if you hate westerns, consider it a morality play set in the west and move them doggies out....<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4748491210829961357" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2526895131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2526895131" />
    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2526895131?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=western-literature">western-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2526895131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17993839533823190955">I started this book thinking I was getting into an adventure/survivalist tale of buffalo hunters in the old west. I turned the last page knowing that, instead, it was an epic tragedy. If you can read this book without having your stomach turned at the senseless slaughter of these animals, you're a stronger person than I am.<br/><br/> "A long, narrow valley, flat as the top of a table, wound among the mountains. Lush grass grew on the bed of the valley, and waved gently in the breeze as far as the eye coul</span>
  <span id="freeText17993839533823190955" style="display:none">I started this book thinking I was getting into an adventure/survivalist tale of buffalo hunters in the old west. I turned the last page knowing that, instead, it was an epic tragedy. If you can read this book without having your stomach turned at the senseless slaughter of these animals, you're a stronger person than I am.<br /><br /> "A long, narrow valley, flat as the top of a table, wound among the mountains. Lush grass grew on the bed of the valley, and waved gently in the breeze as far as the eye could see. A quietness seemed to rise from the valley; it was the quietness, the stillness, the absolute calm of a land where no human foot had touched". <br /><br />Four men go into this valley to kill one of the last of the big herds in the country. They get greedy, get caught in the snows, and stumble out of the valley six months later, leaving behind carnage and destruction. There is much more to this story, of course, and that is where the tragedy lies. It's really too bad that buffalo were such gentle creatures, I was hoping they would turn on the hunters, leaving four dead men instead of thousands of dead buffalo. But no such luck. <br /><br />This was a complex novel of men and their dreams and motives, and what they are willing to do to achieve them. "Stoner", by the same author, is one of my favorite novels ever, but for those who complained that that book was boring, give this one a try. There's plenty of action here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17993839533823190955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1366938291" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1366938291" />
    <a title="Parthiban Sekar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29910674-parthiban-sekar"><img alt="Parthiban Sekar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517476102p2/29910674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1366938291?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Parthiban Sekar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Parthiban Sekar" href="/user/show/29910674-parthiban-sekar">Parthiban Sekar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29910674-parthiban-sekar?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29910674-parthiban-sekar?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1366938291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15599911722746610117"><b>Bulls-eye:</b> <br/>After reading this book, I have felt myself becoming one of those naive victims from this story. At first, I was unaware of what is going to happen amidst the scurry behind shrubs and rocks, and the constant thuds of distant hooves. I was slowly made to believe that everything is normal without knowing what lies beneath. I suppose that I was taken for granted.<br/><br/>Eventually, there was this strange feeling of foreign intervention which made me question my own existence and my very own purp</span>
  <span id="freeText15599911722746610117" style="display:none"><b>Bulls-eye:</b> <br />After reading this book, I have felt myself becoming one of those naive victims from this story. At first, I was unaware of what is going to happen amidst the scurry behind shrubs and rocks, and the constant thuds of distant hooves. I was slowly made to believe that everything is normal without knowing what lies beneath. I suppose that I was taken for granted.<br /><br />Eventually, there was this strange feeling of foreign intervention which made me question my own existence and my very own purpose in this life. There were chaos and confusions everywhere, and bodies hitting the ground at regular intervals. The need to survive shattered our unity. The sunlight revealed the intrusion of hunters, watching with stoic interest, indifferently pulling the trigger, and putting us to an eternal sleep. But, high above, there was the Sun slowly passing behind us, after beating on us all day. Everyday was an equinox of life and death.<br /><br />The onset of the winter drew the men back to their camps and some of our lives were spared. I resumed my old life indifferently, away from the flayed bodies. The men strove against the winter with our hides and survived on our flesh. Several months later, there was no sign of them but the strong stench of the wastes they left behind. <br /><br />It made me wonder that what was that these men were after: were they looking only to earn a living from the hides, or find a meaning which might lie under the hides? <br /><br /><b>Birds-eye</b>:<br />With the lively descriptions of the travel of the hunting party, it was hard not to go along with them to the faraway mountains and the wild prairies. But, a sense of question always kept gnawing at me: what is there to find among buffaloes, that cannot be found among people? The lovely analogy between the undressed harlot and the skinned calf stands out to be some of the best parts I've read so far.<br /><br />So, the way I see it: this inquisitive work of art seems to ask one and only one thing: <i>Is there any meaning, after all?</i><br /><br /><i><b>Disclaimer:</b> Please ignore any silly errors and inferences as I made myself write after a really long time. Nevertheless, try reading this compelling book, may be not from buffalo's perspective like me. Not Joking! Ha Ha!</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15599911722746610117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1275652615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1275652615" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36235386-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590503935p2/36235386.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1275652615?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/36235386-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1275652615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17528177664153047279">I enjoyed this sparse and dark novel set in Kansas and Colorado in the period after the Civil War.Strong characterisation and very atmospheric.This is Moby Dick brought to a rapidly changing American West.A precursor to the novels of Cormac McCarthy.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1807964046" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1807964046" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1807964046?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1807964046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12183312109407052702">People, here and elsewhere, are agrief over the most recent national election. A common question is, "How could this happen?" For the handful of people who are not asking that question rhetorically, I could reply "I don't know" or I could attempt a lengthy answer about how the choice meant different things to different people, different oxen being gored. I'd never finish the longer answer. In the unlikely event that the first or second explanation didn't cause a disagreement, eventually ennui wo</span>
  <span id="freeText12183312109407052702" style="display:none">People, here and elsewhere, are agrief over the most recent national election. A common question is, "How could this happen?" For the handful of people who are not asking that question rhetorically, I could reply "I don't know" or I could attempt a lengthy answer about how the choice meant different things to different people, different oxen being gored. I'd never finish the longer answer. In the unlikely event that the first or second explanation didn't cause a disagreement, eventually ennui would set in, because, really, the explanations would never end. It would be like asking "What is an American?" Maybe like this . . .<br /><br />Four men head west. It's Miller's plan. He thinks he knows where one of the last great herds of buffalo is, hidden through a pass in the Colorado Rockies. With him is Charley Hoge, one-handed, with a need for whiskey and his well-worn Bible, but useful and loyal. Schneider, too is along, a cold, dark presence, a skinner, sharpened distrust. And lastly is Will Andrews, with three years of Harvard in him and bankroll enough. Andrews is searching for . . . something. He doesn't know what. <br /><br />Three of them are on horses, with Charley Hoge on the cart harruping the oxen. Only three horsemen, but their journey will be apocalyptic enough. Miller turns Ahab-like, determined to erase the entire herd. Till Schneider stops, as if he has heard a distant thunder. The killing stops; the men look upward. And there: the first snowflake.<br /><br />All their bloodlust, all their hubris; but they have miscalculated. They have gotten in, but they can not get out. <br /><br />Some hold that the writing of the Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse is a prophecy. And not a rosy one. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/457228.Butcher_s_Crossing" title="Butcher's Crossing by John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener"> Butcher's Crossing</a> was published just as the first troops were landing in Vietnam. I read this as more troops are in Afghanistan mountains. The miscalculation need not be precisely prophesized when the lesson is universal. Nor, I think, is the allegory limited to War.<br /><br />What was Andrews searching for? Another character, McDonald, tells him this:<br /><br /><i>"Well, there's nothing. . . . You get born, and you nurse on lies, and you get weaned on lies, and you learn fancier lies in school. You live all your life on lies, and then maybe when you're ready to die, it comes to you--that there's nothing, nothing but yourself and what you could have done. Only you ain't done it, because the lies told you there was something else. Then you know you could of had the world, because you're the only one that knows the secret; only it's too late. You're too old."</i><br /><br />Andrews protests.<br /><br /><i>"You ain't learned, then," McDonald said. "You ain't learned yet. . ."</i><br /><br />As I was saying, some of you have asked, "How could this happen..........?"<br /><br /><br />-- -- -- -- -- -- -- --<br /><br />And having nothing to do with this book or review......<br /><br />R.I.P. Leon: <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hbUwkXZ4TtE">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hbUwk...</a><br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3312623815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3312623815" />
    <a title="Krista" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4729639-krista"><img alt="Krista" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579967825p2/4729639.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3312623815?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista" href="/user/show/4729639-krista">Krista</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3312623815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7402968033617551561"><i>
  <blockquote>He believed – and had believed for a long time – that there was a subtle magnetism in nature; which if he unconsciously yielded to it, would direct him aright, not indifferent to the way he walked. But he felt that only during the few days that he had been in Butcher’s Crossing had nature been so purely presented to him that its power of compulsion was sufficiently strong to strike through his will, his habit, and his idea. He turned west, his back toward Butcher’s Crossing and the town and c</blockquote></i></span>
  <span id="freeText7402968033617551561" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>He believed – and had believed for a long time – that there was a subtle magnetism in nature; which if he unconsciously yielded to it, would direct him aright, not indifferent to the way he walked. But he felt that only during the few days that he had been in Butcher’s Crossing had nature been so purely presented to him that its power of compulsion was sufficiently strong to strike through his will, his habit, and his idea. He turned west, his back toward Butcher’s Crossing and the town and cities that lay eastward beyond it; he walked past the clump of cottonwoods toward the river he had not seen but which had assumed in his mind the proportions of a vast boundary that lay between himself and the wildness and freedom that his instinct sought.</blockquote>
</i><br />Alas, I have now read the last of John Williams' three profoundly perfect and completely different novels – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/166997.Stoner" title="Stoner by John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Stoner</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89231.Augustus" title="Augustus by John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Augustus</a>, and now <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/457228.Butcher_s_Crossing" title="Butcher's Crossing by John Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Butcher's Crossing</a> – and I'd say that if they have anything in common it would be a quest to know one's self and one's connection to the rest of humanity (and is there any topic more appropriate or even <i>vital</i> for fiction to explore?) In the current case, Williams (as it states in the introduction to <i>Butcher's Crossing</i>) uses the tropes of a western – “an excavation <i>of</i> cliche and not a participation <i>in</i> cliche” – in order to explore one young man's coming of age, set against the backdrop of America's coming of age. In each case – whether confronting the truth at the heart of a man or of a country – there are lies and myths and misunderstanding to be overcome before the future may be met with integrity. Coming as it did in 1960 – when the movies still portrayed the Plains-clearing cowboys as the good guys, when the Greatest Generation went from World War to Cold War, when an American president could extend his country's claims of manifest destiny out into space – this questioning of American exceptionalism must have come as quite a shock. Reading it today, the specific material isn't so paradigm-challenging, but as it deals with perpetual truths and the search for meaning and authenticity, <i>Butcher's Crossing</i> is still important, engaging, and enduring. (Spoilerish for the uninitiated.) <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>A phrase from a lecture from Mr. Emerson that he had attended came to him: I become a transparent eyeball. Gathered in by field and wood, he was nothing; he saw all; the current of some nameless force circulated through him. And in a way that he could not feel in King's Chapel, in the college rooms, or on the Cambridge streets, he was a part and parcel of God, free and uncontained. Through the trees and across the rolling landscape, he had been able to see a hint of the distant horizon to the west; and there, for an instant, he had beheld somewhat as beautiful as his own undiscovered nature.</blockquote>
</i><br />Although the son of a Unitarian lay minister, Will Andrews was raised more on the writings of Ralph Waldo Emerson than those in the Bible, and so when he found himself the recipient of a decent inheritance at twenty-three, Andrews left his third year of studies at Harvard in order to “find himself in the great West”. Winding up in the small town of Butcher's Crossing, Kansas – led there by a letter of introduction written by Andrews' father to a Mr. McDonald; a hide broker and the only family acquaintance out west – Andrews is quickly directed to a Mr. Miller: a hunter and rugged mountain man who says that although the local Plains had been nearly depleted of buffalo, he knew of the location of the last great herd up in the Colorado mountains, and if a man were to back an expedition to hunt them, that man would make his fortune. Andrews seems more interested in experiencing the authentic West than in actually participating in a buffalo hunt or making money (McDonald offered him safe opportunities for each), and as both Miller and his grizzled, one-armed sidekick seem to represent some kind of Emersonian ideal, Andrews quickly lays down the money for the expedition.<br /><br />As the party (these three men plus a skilled skinner named Schneider, along with a wagon, ox team, and horses) head for the Colorado Territory, Miller begins to exhibit a kind of crazed single-mindedness for his mythologised buffalo herd that seems to telegraph tragedy: this is the old man determined to bring back the massive marlin without regard to his own safety; this is Captain Ahab pursuing the great white whale no matter how many lives are lost. As the party soon crosses a great wasteland – going forward without water at Miller's insistence while there would still have been a slim chance for survival if they turned back towards the river a day's walk behind them – the reader recognises this as hubris and can only wait for divine punishment; even at this point I knew that there <i>must</i> be losses and I constantly mused to myself what might be appropriate – to Williams' credit, it all plays out with perfect justness.<br /><br />Although there may be some question about the existence of the buffalo herd on the journey out – it had, after all, been nearly a decade since Miller last saw it; could he even find the remote valley after so long? – the party eventually does come upon the buffalo in their milling thousands; a scene straight from the early, untouched days of the American West. The beauty and timelessness of the valley is both Biblical and Emersonian in its remove from ordinary experience; but if Andrews had hoped to meet himself upon such terms, what he discovers is something else as the slaughter begins:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>He came to see Miller as a mechanism, an automaton, moved by the moving herd; and he came to see Miller's destruction of the buffalo, not as a lust for blood or a lust for the hides or a lust for what the hides would bring, or even at last the blind lust of fury that toiled darkly within him – he came to see the destruction as a cold, mindless response to the life in which Miller had immersed himself. And he looked upon himself, crawling dumbly after Miller upon the flat bed of the valley, picking up the empty cartridges that he spent, tugging the water keg, husbanding the rifle, cleaning it, offering it to Miller when he needed it – he looked upon himself, and did not know who he was, or where he went.</blockquote>
</i><br />The plot from here plays out with exciting details, to end more or less where I thought it needed to go; but the plot is only the framework upon which Williams pins his philosophy. So what of young Andrews' transformation? Despite forswearing bodily comforts (and especially urban living with its painted lamps and velvet sofas), Andrews can't help but be focused on his body as he discovers that living close to nature is painful, brutish, and detaches him from life – more than once, conditions force time and space to pass Andrews by as though he were on a treadmill, as though he were not present in his life at all; surely this isn't what Emerson saw in a communion with nature? <br /><br />Repeatedly, Andrews feels as though his body is emerging from a cocoon into his authentic self – riding out on his horse, “He thought at times that he was moving into a new body, or into a real body that had lain hidden beneath unreal layers of softness and whiteness and smoothness”; he emerges from an envelope of buffalo skins after a blizzard, “When at last he was able to look around him, he viewed a world that he had not seen before”; he bathes for the first time in eight months, “Watching in a kind of ecstasy the dirt fall away in long strips beneath the gritty soap...marveling at the whiteness of his skin, and slapping it to see the rosy welts appear there” – but although Andrews is frequently reborn bodily through these experiences, he is forever upset by the sight of bare and defenseless carnality. He runs away from a beautiful woman when she offers him her nakedness; he retches at his first sight of a skinned buffalo ready for butchering; he turns away in distaste when he sees his lover sleeping, unguarded and “ugly”. But if he shies away from the body and his transformation is to be a purely mental one, Andrews is further disturbed to have seen each of the men he's dealt with turn into (if only briefly) blank-staring automatons by their face-offs with nature and its deadly forces; could this <i>really</i> be all the fabled West had to offer? Near the end, McDonald gives Andrews this view:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>You get born, and you nurse on lies, and you get weaned on lies, and you learn fancier lies at school. You live all your life on lies, and then maybe when you're ready to die, it comes to you that there's nothing, nothing but yourself and what you could have done. Only you ain't done it, because the lies told you there was something else. Then you know you could of had the world, because you're the only one that knows the secret; only then it's too late. You're too old. </blockquote>
</i><br />Andrews is horrified by this speech – and mostly, I think, because McDonald seems to be talking about money and power and these things aren't really of interest to the young man; he <i>knows</i> that he gained more on the expedition than buffalo skins, even if he can't articulate it – and his repudiation of McDonald seems like a repudiation of American exceptionalism and its foundational myths. When Andrews is ready to leave Butcher's Crossing at the end, it's with maturity and clear vision; he is finally able to engage with the world as it is, as a transparent eyeball, unsullied by preconceptions of what that world should look like.<br /><br />The writing in <i>Butcher's Crossing</i> is in the sweeping Western style, and read as just another entry in that genre, one might think it simply an interesting look at the last great buffalo hunt. But John Williams was a master of bending genre to his service and this tale is more Homer than Hondo; it cuts like sawgrass and lingers longer than a prairie sunset but it deals with hubris and hamartia and retribution from gods Olympian. Absolutely loved reading this and thinking about it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7402968033617551561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2806211328?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=western">western</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2806211328" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14103162773856268886">There are parts of Butcher's Crossing that remind me of another book or story, or maybe it is actual history I think of. It's the “go west, young man”, kind of saying that you've probably heard at some point yourself. The young man's name in this story is Andrews. His reasoning at this time in his life is the same as many others have been and will be: abandon the former life and future for the open and untamed West that calls to him. My synopsis of Andrews' initial plight is quite up to par to h</span>
  <span id="freeText14103162773856268886" style="display:none">There are parts of Butcher's Crossing that remind me of another book or story, or maybe it is actual history I think of. It's the “go west, young man”, kind of saying that you've probably heard at some point yourself. The young man's name in this story is Andrews. His reasoning at this time in his life is the same as many others have been and will be: abandon the former life and future for the open and untamed West that calls to him. My synopsis of Andrews' initial plight is quite up to par to his wording. His would be far more eloquent. But of course those are John William's words on the page. Not easy to equal, are his words - simply gorgeous to me. While reading Butcher's Crossing, I remembered Stoner, the other Williams' book I read. Stoner also left his would-be-path for a new one, but besides the writing it's the only other comparison.<br /><br />Along with three other men, William Andrews finds what he was looking for in the West, at least in part, in the Buffalo herds that roam the plains - as did so many men of that history. But with it they also find what they couldn't have bargained for. To me, this book read like the hand of fate. The answer to the compulsion of one man, the greed of another, and innocence changed. In many ways, John Williams turns the western on it's head, and does it so well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14103162773856268886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1365305685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1365305685" />
    <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson"><img alt="Betsy Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404493492p2/5149179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1365305685?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Betsy Robinson" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson">Betsy Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1365305685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11415020628747567087"><br/>In the 1870s, Will Andrews, a young man fresh out of three years at Harvard College, travels west and, with a band of hunters, heads further west in search of buffalo because he is seeking Nature (intentional capital “N”).<br/><blockquote>Andrews felt that the mountains drew them onward, and drew them with increasing intensity as they came nearer, as if they were a giant lodestone whose influence increased to the degree that it was more nearly approached. As they came nearer he had again the feeling that he was </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11415020628747567087" style="display:none"><br />In the 1870s, Will Andrews, a young man fresh out of three years at Harvard College, travels west and, with a band of hunters, heads further west in search of buffalo because he is seeking Nature (intentional capital “N”).<br /><blockquote>Andrews felt that the mountains drew them onward, and drew them with increasing intensity as they came nearer, as if they were a giant lodestone whose influence increased to the degree that it was more nearly approached. As they came nearer he had again the feeling that he was being absorbed, included in something with which he had had no relation before; but unlike the feeling of absorption he had experienced on the anonymous prairie, this feeling was one which promised, however vaguely, a richness and a fulfillment for which he had no name. (106)</blockquote><br />This is the best description of my experience reading this book that I can imagine. <br /><br />I am a vegan. I don’t proselytize; I feed my dog meat and am grateful that there are people who are willing to slaughter the lambs so that she can eat them. But I’m most comfortable living in a way that causes the least amount of harm to other sentient beings. So it was with some discomfort that I decided to read about a buffalo hunt. I won’t lie. It was difficult. I felt sick to my stomach reading about the preparations to slaughter—for their hides—an entire herd of animals, trapped in a valley. But I kept reading because I trust that if John Williams wrote it, it’s about more than violence. But that was another reservation—my trust is almost reverential: I so revere his book <i>Stoner</i>—I’ve read it twice and am now in the process of studying it chapter by chapter—that, in a way, I was nervous about being let down.<br /><br />There is no let-down in <i>Butcher’s Crossing</i>. The man writes with a pure life energy and depth of understanding, along with skill, flawless technique, and such visceral descriptions that, for a lot of this book I was in pain. Just when I thought I could not physically endure what I was feeling during the killing, there came relief: <blockquote>During the last hour of the stand he [Andrews] came to see Miller’s destruction of the buffalo, not as a lust for blood or a lust for the hides or a lust for what the hides would bring, or even at last the blind lust of fury that toiled darkly within him—he came to see the destruction as a cold, mindless response to the life in which Miller had immersed himself. And he looked upon himself, crawling dumbly after Miller upon the flat bed of the valley, picking up the empty cartridges that he spent, tugging the water keg, husbanding the rifle, cleaning it, offering it to Miller when he needed it—he looked upon himself, and did not know who he was, or where he went. (137)</blockquote><br />A transcendent point of view of the horror of disconnection from Source—a disconnection that is a particular malady of non-Native people; this was a White man’s hunt and slaughter and devaluing of or blindness to life. <br /><br />And after the evening of butchery, just when I thought I might throw up, an understanding of the horror: <blockquote>It came to him that he had turned away from the buffalo not because of a womanish nausea at blood and stench and spilling gut; it came to him that he had sickened and turned away because of his shock at seeing the buffalo, a few moments before proud and noble and full of the dignity of life, now stark and helpless, a length of inert meat, divested of itself, or his notion of its self, swinging grotesquely, mockingly, before him. It was not itself; or it was not that self that he had imagined it to be. That self was murdered; and in that murder he had felt the destruction of something within him, and he had not been able to face it. So he had turned away. (151)</blockquote><br />I’ve never been a terribly athletic person or one who is drawn to daredevil activities, but I understand the spiritual journey of “using yourself up” and thereby experiencing your essence. Although I find quite enough uninvited pain in just living, I understand the draw of everything from Outward Bound survival experiences to the Native American Sundance—where people go to their physical limits and maybe even beyond. But in all these rituals, the pain is experienced by the person who chose it: there is the risk of death, bearing unbearable pain. In Williams’s <i>Butcher’s Crossing</i>, yes, the men endure the violence of nature and weather and hunger and thirst. But the real violence is what they wreak on others—from the horses and oxen who serve them to the hundreds of buffalo they slaughter for (SPOILER) no apparent purpose other than the ritual of it. <br /><br />I read the last thirty pages of this book in a state of breathlessness. How would this end? My worst fear was that it would turn into a romantic noble cowboy fantasy. Then, when Andrews joined with a woman, I worried that it would devolve into some pat message about balanced male/female energies. Neither of those things happened. I will not spoil the ending. It is not a warm, comforting message, but it is true. My faith and trust in John Williams are validated.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11415020628747567087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Håkon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49126210-h-kon"><img alt="Håkon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578925230p2/49126210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2890210747?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Håkon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Håkon" href="/user/show/49126210-h-kon">Håkon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2890210747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12391295430817364511">Rating 86/100<br/><br/><br/>John Williams' prose is simple and sharp and meaningful. His imagery is vivid. You can almost hear the horse hooves and the Wagon creaking in the ground as they travel through Colorado. I once watched a video essay on the films of Andrei Tarkovsky, the Russian filmmaker, in which the essayist argued that Tarkovsky's strength lied in texture and sound. I think this is very much the case with Butcher's Crossing as well.<br/><br/>It's an American western about human greed, about destruction, abo</span>
  <span id="freeText12391295430817364511" style="display:none">Rating 86/100<br /><br /><br />John Williams' prose is simple and sharp and meaningful. His imagery is vivid. You can almost hear the horse hooves and the Wagon creaking in the ground as they travel through Colorado. I once watched a video essay on the films of Andrei Tarkovsky, the Russian filmmaker, in which the essayist argued that Tarkovsky's strength lied in texture and sound. I think this is very much the case with Butcher's Crossing as well.<br /><br />It's an American western about human greed, about destruction, about faith and dreams and love. Most importantly it's about the Buffalo, and America's abuse of them. It is also clearly about capitalism and the profit-motive. <br /><br />John Williams tells the story of a town that flew too close to the sun. A town that was enamored with greed and excess. Butcher's Crossing, the town, is very much like our own civilization, which is mighty near the sun.<br /><br />Butcher's Crossing is completely different from Stoner. Butcher's Crossing is a western. I'd argue that it's a very good Western. A "literary" one. It thumps, and loops and thirsts through what remains of the American wilderness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12391295430817364511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14047887744519152850">Reading this book was like watching an archer shoot an arrow but in an ultra slow motion.<br/><br/>Like watching those eyes squint at the target, the shooting arm pulling back the arrow while slowly building up the power in the tightening muscles, keeping the spectator transfixed and waiting for the moment of truth. And then it comes. The release of the arrow sans any blasting sound. Swoosh. Bull's eye.<br/><br/>Yep, John Williams can pull a punch. That too with glorious simplicity. You realize later that you have </span>
  <span id="freeText14047887744519152850" style="display:none">Reading this book was like watching an archer shoot an arrow but in an ultra slow motion.<br /><br />Like watching those eyes squint at the target, the shooting arm pulling back the arrow while slowly building up the power in the tightening muscles, keeping the spectator transfixed and waiting for the moment of truth. And then it comes. The release of the arrow sans any blasting sound. Swoosh. Bull's eye.<br /><br />Yep, John Williams can pull a punch. That too with glorious simplicity. You realize later that you have been profoundly hit and you can do nothing about it. You find yourself submitting to it completely and absolutely. And after he is done, it becomes hard for you to recover and accept the other reality. The reality no more being shown to you in his mirror, but the one that you now have to endure.</span>

<div id="review_19339676" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19339676" />
    <a title="Sarah Funke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/170563-sarah-funke"><img alt="Sarah Funke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1192709674p2/170563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19339676?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Funke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Funke" href="/user/show/170563-sarah-funke">Sarah Funke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19339676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11803503372880751246">Anything that's prefaced by a quotation from Melville's The Confidence Man is ok by me. This one, by the author of Stoner, owes a lot more to Melville than that -- from Transcendental meditations to musings on "white," not to mention the monomania of a man on a hunt (like Gatsby, come to think of it, but broader, and deeper, and colder/wetter, and hotter/thirstier). This is Moby-Dick in the early American west, with many, many buffalo instead of a single whale, and set very very much on land. Wi</span>
  <span id="freeText11803503372880751246" style="display:none">Anything that's prefaced by a quotation from Melville's The Confidence Man is ok by me. This one, by the author of Stoner, owes a lot more to Melville than that -- from Transcendental meditations to musings on "white," not to mention the monomania of a man on a hunt (like Gatsby, come to think of it, but broader, and deeper, and colder/wetter, and hotter/thirstier). This is Moby-Dick in the early American west, with many, many buffalo instead of a single whale, and set very very much on land. Williams's writing doesn't lend itself to easy quotation -- he does not write epigrammatically, but gorgeously. I'd imagine it would be a wonderful book to read aloud (if anyone still does that). The parts that I marked to re-read and re-read are paragraphs, passages, and pages, so I'll leave it to you to get a copy, open it to any page, and see if you can put it down. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11803503372880751246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review19339676">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_910780375" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/910780375" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/910780375?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099589679" href="/book/show/18395049-butcher-s-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=lit">lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer910780375" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6809530911316840264">John 'Stoner' Williams' bleak and unromantic portrait of the great myth of the Western Frontier is a hard edged read designed to repulse the reader with its content whilst wallowing in the majesty of nature. It's no mean feat to capture such beauty and such horror in one novel with equal skill and success, in doing so Williams confirms his place in my heart as one the greats of American letters, and if justice is served all of us who care for the careful consideration of how one word follows ano</span>
  <span id="freeText6809530911316840264" style="display:none">John 'Stoner' Williams' bleak and unromantic portrait of the great myth of the Western Frontier is a hard edged read designed to repulse the reader with its content whilst wallowing in the majesty of nature. It's no mean feat to capture such beauty and such horror in one novel with equal skill and success, in doing so Williams confirms his place in my heart as one the greats of American letters, and if justice is served all of us who care for the careful consideration of how one word follows another to create effect and analyse what makes us Human will come to feel the same way.<br /><br /><i>”Day by day the numbness crept upon him until at last the numbness seemed to be himself. He felt himself to be like the land, without identity or shape; sometimes one of the men would look at him, look through him, as if he did not exist; and he had to shake his head sharply and move an arm or a leg and glance at it to assure himself that he was visible.”</i><br /><br />This numbness experienced by the four main characters right at the start of their journey to slaughter four thousand increasingly scarce buffalo only grows through the following three hundred pages, evolves in to a loss of understanding of Living as the men devolve, they enter a period of their lives where the passage of time is no longer something to consider, mere structure for the repetition forced upon them by their unnatural assault on nature and insanity creeps in to proceedings, insanity that some will succumb to for the final time whilst others manage to recognise it for what it is and fight back.<br /><br />You're left with the impression that these hardships were accurate portraits of everyday existence in the early 1870s, that Williams perfectly captures the zeitgeist of the place and the period, at once dispelling the romantic myth, the same one that propels William Andrews to leave Harvard and search for himself out on the Buffalo Plains, and creating another form of it for writers such as Cormac McCarthy to mine in brutal detail a little later in the twentieth century.<br /><br />It's a tough read at times, I can't imagine anybody would claim to want to read a novel about the eradication of a species for entertainment value, we all know it happened but that doesn't detract from its value as a representation of our shared history, just look at the choices made by Steve McQueen in his Academy Award winning adaptation of 12 Years A Slave for a similar experience. It's a piece of art and art doesn't have to be comfortable for the consumer. In addition to the reprehensible behaviour of the protagonists Williams' attempts to convey the effect of isolation on the passage of time are somehow reflected in the reading, there were times when I thought I'd been reading for hours but only minutes had past such was the exhausting and draining nature of the events. Powerful stuff but not something I can imagine anyone sitting down to reread with any regularity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6809530911316840264" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
