<div id="review_2862374967" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2862374967" />
    <a title="TBV" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98589549-tbv"><img alt="TBV" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560644618p2/98589549.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2862374967?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TBV" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TBV" href="/user/show/98589549-tbv">TBV</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11129189-butcher-s-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98589549-tbv?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2862374967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10325316818157788048">Is the sighting of large herds of buffalo on a hidden track nine or ten years previously good enough reason to set off on a buffalo killing expedition? Miller thinks so, and young Andrews is keen to join, and he needs no persuading to both invest money and join as learner skinner. But this is no simple adventure story.<br/><br/>Why is Miller, leader of the expedition, so obsessed with finding this herd and killing every single bison in it? Why does the fanatically religious Charley tag along almost agains</span>
  <span id="freeText10325316818157788048" style="display:none">Is the sighting of large herds of buffalo on a hidden track nine or ten years previously good enough reason to set off on a buffalo killing expedition? Miller thinks so, and young Andrews is keen to join, and he needs no persuading to both invest money and join as learner skinner. But this is no simple adventure story.<br><br>Why is Miller, leader of the expedition, so obsessed with finding this herd and killing every single bison in it? Why does the fanatically religious Charley tag along almost against his will? What is it that compels Schneider the master skinner? What is it that Will Andrews, newly arrived from Boston is looking for? These are Will's thoughts early in the novel: <i>“But whatever he spoke he knew would be but another name for the wildness that he sought. It was a freedom and a goodness, a hope and a vigor that he perceived to underlie all the familiar things of his life, which were not free or good or hopeful or vigorous. What he sought was the source and preserver of his world, a world which seemed to turn ever in fear away from its source, rather than search it out, as the prairie grass around him sent down its fibered roots into the rich dark dampness, the Wildness, and thereby renewed itself, year after year."</i> What will the expedition bring? During the course of their travels Andrews often reflects on his reasons for being there: <i> "Before, his eyes had remained for most of the time fixed upon Miller’s back; now they strained into the distance, toward the uneven hump of earth, now sharp, now blurred, upon the far horizon. And he found that he hungered after them much as he had thirsted after the water; but he knew the mountains were there, he could see them; and he did not know precisely what hunger or thirst they would assuage.”</i> and "... <i>he looked upon himself, and did not know who he was, or where he went.</i>“ What will Andrews discover about himself and his partners?<br><br>John Williams takes us through the minutiae of their journey, with magnificent descriptions of the landscape, the agony and exhaustion of the men, the contented sound (or not) of the livestock and at times the silence. Beauty and horror exist side by side here. Stupidity and greed take over as Miller obsessively decimates the vast herd as if they were goldfish in a bowl. Piles of corpses are left rotting and stinking. But that is not all that is rotten... And then it seems that nature itself steps in to avenge the mass slaughter.<br><br>This is a thought provoking novel, and words that kept popping into my head were 'arrogance', 'stupidity', 'greed', 'futility' and 'illusion'.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562903413i/27813611._SX540_.jpg" alt=" photo 2000-07-04 00.00.00-32_zpsophxhsu1.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="10325316818157788048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2413846083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18023544501737680918"> I started this review during my reading of the book and it is just a list of thoughts, feelings and emotions really but I'll go with it because that's how it is. <br/><br/>1870s Kansas The wild west home to Prospectors, Buffalos and buffalo hunters Butchers crossing, a small place, appears to be there specifically for the needs of the buffalo hunters to return to ,to sell the hides of those majestic animals.<br/><br/>William Andrews is the main protagonist . Andrews wishes to have experiences closer to nature, and</span>
  <span id="freeText18023544501737680918" style="display:none"> I started this review during my reading of the book and it is just a list of thoughts, feelings and emotions really but I'll go with it because that's how it is. <br /><br />1870s Kansas The wild west home to Prospectors, Buffalos and buffalo hunters Butchers crossing, a small place, appears to be there specifically for the needs of the buffalo hunters to return to ,to sell the hides of those majestic animals.<br /><br />William Andrews is the main protagonist . Andrews wishes to have experiences closer to nature, and so he comes to the town of Butcher’s Crossing, in search of adventure and hoping, naively to find his "unalterable self". <br /><br />Within the pages these characters, and because of John Williams incredible ability as an author, I myself went through the full range of emotions and changes such as, obsession, loss of reason,loss of self, desensitisation, fear and more. <br />Now this book isn't an easy read and there are scenes of slaughter which are almost intolerable, and you will learn in great and vivid detail how to skin a buffalo. But for all that it is a book which exemplifies the human psyche like nothing else I've ever read. <br /><br />This book is for me perfectly written and I maybe like it better than Stoner. <br />Huge 5 * from me.</span>

<div id="review_2096312056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2096312056" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2096312056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12293933714022712589">It feels strange to say such a raw and even grubby book is such a delight. While deeply unsettling in the end with the main character a changed nomad it is a perfect book for its pitch and character dynamics. As I sit on a little plane flying back from Northern NSW looking at a world of fluffy clouds I think of the snowed in scenes of “Butchers Crossing” and the scene of white blindness. <br/>Williams is a fabulous writer. I have only read a little poetry but his awe of nature reminds me of Gerard Ma</span>
  <span id="freeText12293933714022712589" style="display:none">It feels strange to say such a raw and even grubby book is such a delight. While deeply unsettling in the end with the main character a changed nomad it is a perfect book for its pitch and character dynamics. As I sit on a little plane flying back from Northern NSW looking at a world of fluffy clouds I think of the snowed in scenes of “Butchers Crossing” and the scene of white blindness. <br />Williams is a fabulous writer. I have only read a little poetry but his awe of nature reminds me of Gerard Manley Hopkins .</span>

<div id="review_1934443796" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1934443796" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1934443796?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099589679" href="/book/show/18395049-butcher-s-crossing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=5-star-fiction">5-star-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1934443796" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14754773002456896633">A few months ago a family member recommended “Stoner” to me. I wasn’t sure if that was my sort of book so thought I would introduce myself to John Williams’s work via “Butcher’s Crossing”. <br/> <br/>We are in the 1870s where a young Bostonian, Will Andrews, drops out of Harvard and heads west. Andrews is one of those people who believe that true wisdom and meaning can only be found in the wilderness. He ends up financing, and being part of, a buffalo hunt led by a man called Miller. The latter is experie</span>
  <span id="freeText14754773002456896633" style="display:none">A few months ago a family member recommended “Stoner” to me. I wasn’t sure if that was my sort of book so thought I would introduce myself to John Williams’s work via “Butcher’s Crossing”. <br /> <br />We are in the 1870s where a young Bostonian, Will Andrews, drops out of Harvard and heads west. Andrews is one of those people who believe that true wisdom and meaning can only be found in the wilderness. He ends up financing, and being part of, a buffalo hunt led by a man called Miller. The latter is experienced and knowledgeable about surviving in the wild, and is calm and rational in a crisis, but he is also a man with a touch of madness about him. With the buffalo herds being rapidly exterminated, Miller believes he knows of a “last valley”, a sort of untouched Eden that no other hunters have found. One of the main themes is about what happens when humans encounter wilderness. In this novel, each takes a heavy toll of the other.<br /><br />A top-class novel, with a strong plot, atmospheric settings and convincing characterisations. There’s a lot of detailed description, but the novel never gets boring. Deservedly a classic.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14754773002456896633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7/wgaAsM1v8ttSW7SHyM3ewdNZchNqgxcKMH8qkGtA/trR6/tepJ4JLArj6AxX6O0JuWliNntyXBCdwvhOMj/A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EmtLus9eBBY84gGsCxyEnm9sc7FpE5s7iia6uDri71oQOnVtcbibCYOXiinDpXbNU+rQsGtChC87jGFlFwd4qQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It tells the story of two young boxers out of Stockton, California: Ernie Munger and Billy Tully, one in his late teens, the other just turning thirty, whose seemingly parallel lives intersect for a time. Set in an ambiance of glittering dreams and drab realities, it tells of the two fighters\' struggles to escape the confinements of their existence, and of the men and women in their world. Fat City is a novel about the sporting life like no other ever written: without melodrama or false heroics, written with a truthfulness that is at once painful and beautiful.\n\nDenis Johnson: &quot;Between the ages of 19 and 25 I studied Leonard Gardner’s book so closely that I began to fear I’d never be able to write anything but imitations of it, so I swore it off(...)When I was about 34 (the same age Gardner was when he published his), my first novel came out. About a year later I borrowed Fat City from the library and read it. I could see immediately that ten years’ exile hadn’t saved me from the influence of its perfection — I’d taught myself to write in Gardner’s style, though not as well. And now, many years later, it’s still true: Leonard Gardner has something to say in every word I write.&quot;\n\nJoan Didion: &quot;Leonard Gardner\'s Fat City affected me more than any new fiction I have read in a long while, and I do not think it affected me only because I come from Fat City, or somewhere near it. He has got it exactly right--the hanging around gas stations, the field dust, the relentless oppressiveness of the weather, the bleak liaisons sealed on the levees and Greyhound buses--but he has done more than just get it down, he has made it a metaphor for the joyless in heart.&quot;\n\nDavid Wagoner: &quot;The people he writes about are alive and three-dimensional, and have that meaty, sweaty immediacy I admire in novels and find so seldom. It\'s an odd, interesting world he explores here--as tense and vivid as the prose.&quot;\n\nIvan Gold: &quot;Gardner writes with power, with an insider\'s knowledge, and with a vividness and love for his characters which redeem them even when they\'re lost and beaten.&quot;\n\nHarry Mark Petrakis: &quot;A man of real talent. He makes the savage world he writes of come alive to the point where the reader can smell the sweat, and feel the anguish of unremitting failure.&quot;\n\nRoss Macdonald: &quot;In his pity and art Gardner moves beyond race, beyond guilt and punishment, as Twain and Melville did, into a tragic forgiveness. I have seldom read a novel as beautiful and individual as this one.&quot;\n\n\nOriginally published in 1969, Fat City is an American classic whose stature has increased over the years. The first male child born in the newly established Republic of Texas, Eli McCullough is thirteen years old when a marauding band of Comanche storm his homestead and brutally murder his mother and sister, taking him captive. Brave and clever, Eli quickly adapts to Comanche life, learning their ways and language, answering to a new name, carving a place as the chief\'s adopted son, and waging war against their enemies, including white men-complicating his sense of loyalty and understanding of who he is. But when disease, starvation, and overwhelming numbers of armed Americans decimate the tribe, Eli finds himself alone. Neither white nor Indian, civilized or fully wild, he must carve a place for himself in a world in which he does not fully belong-a journey of adventure, tragedy, hardship, grit, and luck that reverberates in the lives of his progeny. \n\nIntertwined with Eli\'s story are those of his son, Peter, a man who bears the emotional cost of his father\'s drive for power, and JA, Eli\'s great-granddaughter, a woman who must fight hardened rivals to succeed in a man\'s world.\n\nPhillipp Meyer deftly explores how Eli\'s ruthlessness and steely pragmatism transform subsequent generations of McCulloughs. Love, honor, children are sacrificed in the name of ambition, as the family becomes one of the richest powers in Texas, a ranching-and-oil dynasty of unsurpassed wealth and privilege. Yet, like all empires, the McCoulloughs must eventually face the consequences of their choices.\n\nHarrowing, panoramic, and vividly drawn, The Son is a masterful achievement from a sublime young A little overloaded, perhaps—the oddly assorted cargo includes piles of old newspapers and heaps of tobacco—the ship departs for the Panama Canal from Norfolk, Virginia, on a beautiful autumn day. Before long, the weather turns unexpectedly rough—rougher in fact than even the most experienced members of the crew have ever encountered. The Archimedes, it turns out, has been swept up in the vortex of an immense hurricane, and for the next four days it will be battered and mauled by wind and waves as it is driven wildly off course. Caught in an unremitting struggle for survival, both the crew and the ship will be tested as never before.Based on detailed research into an actual event, Richard Hughes’s tale of high suspense on the high seas is an extraordinary story of men under pressure and the unexpected ways they prove their mettle—or crack. Yet the originality, art, and greatness of In Hazard stem from something else: Hughes’s eerie fascination with the hurricane itself, the inhuman force around which this wrenching tale of humanity at its limits revolves. Hughes channels the furies of sea and sky into a piece of writing that is both apocalyptic and analytic. First comes a trespassing Indian and the dream of wolves running wild amongst the cattle lately brought onto the plain by settlers - this when all the wisdom of trappers has disappeared along with the trappers themselves. So Billy sets forth at the age of sixteen on an unwitting journey into the souls of boys, animals and men.\n\nHaving trapped a she-wolf he would restore to the mountains of Mexico, he is long gone and returns to find everything he left behind transformed utterly in his absence. One brother finds his destiny, while the other arrives only at his fate.\n\nAn essential novel by any measure, and the transfixing middle passage of Cormac McCarthy\'s ongoing trilogy, The Crossing is       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/51229.John_Williams">About John  Williams</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/51229.John_Williams"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1519832171p3/51229.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/51229.John_Williams">John  Williams
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4020788511776613537"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name.</i><br/><br/><b>John Edward Williams</b>, Ph.D. (University of Missouri, 1954; M.A., University of Denver, 1950; B.A., U. of D., 1949), enlisted in the USAAF early in 1942, spending two and a half years as a sergeant in India and Burma. His first novel, <i>Nothing But the Night</i>, was published in 1948, and his first volume of poems, <i>Th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4020788511776613537" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name.</i><br /><br /><b>John Edward Williams</b>, Ph.D. (University of Missouri, 1954; M.A., University of Denver, 1950; B.A., U. of D., 1949), enlisted in the USAAF early in 1942, spending two and a half years as a sergeant in India and Burma. His first novel, <i>Nothing But the Night</i>, was published in 1948, and his first volume of poems, <i>The Broken Landscape</i>, appeared the following year.<br /><br />In the fall of 1955, Williams took over the directorship of the creative writing program at the University of Denver, where he taught for more than 30 years.<br /><br />After retiring from the University of Denver in 1986, Williams moved with his wife, Nancy, to Fayetteville, Arkansas, where he resided until he died of respiratory failure on March 3, 1994. A fifth novel, <i>The Sleep of Reason</i>, was left unfinished at the time of his death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4020788511776613537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/445754-butcher-s-crossing">Quotes from Butcher&#39;s Crossing</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Young people," McDonald said contemptuously. "You always think there's something to find out."<br /><br />"Yes, sir," Andrews said.<br /><br />"Well, there's nothing," McDonald said. "You get born, and you nurse on lies, and you get weaned on lies, and you learn fancier lies in school. You live all your life on lies, and then maybe when you're ready to die, it comes to you — that there's nothing, nothing but yourself and what you could have done. Only you ain't done it, because the lies told you there was something else. Then you know you could of had the world, because you're the only one that knows the secret; only then it's too late. You're too old."<br /><br />"No," Andrews said. A vague terror crept from the darkness that surrounded them, and tightened his voice. "That's not the way it is."<br /><br />"You ain't learned, then," McDonald said. "You ain't learned yet. . . .&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/352667-young-people-mcdonald-said-contemptuously-you-always-think-there-s-something">57 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It came to him that he had turned away from the buffalo not because of a womanish nausea at blood and stench and spilling gut; it came to him that he had sickened and turned away because of his shock at seeing the buffalo, a few moments before proud and noble and full of the dignity of life, now stark and helpless, a length of inert meat, divested of itself, or his notion of its self, swinging grotesquely, mockingly, before him. It was not itself; or it was not that self that he had imagined it to be. That self was murdered; and in that murder he had felt the destruction of something within him, and he had not been able to face it. So he had turned away.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/580888-it-came-to-him-that-he-had-turned-away-from">18 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/445754-butcher-s-crossing">More quotes&hellip;</a>
